HE Oregon is home again. After
*, -Â¦"' an absence of-, more than three
Â¦ years, ; into which has been
, I . . crowded achievements that ' will
'â¢^~- : , insure her -lasting fame and an
honorable place in tlie history of

which she set forth spick and span and
untried, j She sailed away to face perhaps
an entire Spanish fleet single handed, and
San Franciscans, proud and fond of the
great ship, waited with unfaltering faith
In her prowess. She returns, and now it
is; the nation that is fond and proud and
happy to welcome her home to her native
port. _ ' . ' . -
"VVith a consistency in keeping with the
nickname of .the "Bulldog of the Navy,"
she added another to her list of surpris
ing feats and appeared off the Golden
Gate most unexpectedly, while a com
mittee of public officials was in meeting,
eagerly planning a reception worthy of
the greatest of battleships. Her arrival,
yesterday afternoon was a surprise, but
the .'deep- voiced chorus of a thousand
whistles bade her welcome, and many a
ferveiit ''God bless her!" arose from the
multitudes who, along the water front,
and on hilltops, watched her stately prog-,
fess Into the harbor. .
j The news, received by telephone from
the Merchants' Exchange, that the Ore
gon had been sighted' seven miles out,
took Mayor Phelan's committee com
pletely by surprise. This was about 3
o'clockT" "All "plans were at once dropped
! and'/. Mayor Phelan ; and t the :. committee ,

<?rnor Markbam' and welcome back the
battleship and her crew. , -
'â¢ It"did~ not "take" long for the'news that
tho; Oregon 1 was "passing in" ; to ; spread,
end as she passed along the front every
wharf was black with "a" cheering crowd,
while every steamship in port and every
factory in the vicinity of the bay had its
whistle golrig. "When the warship was
within half a mile 1 of the battleship Iowa,
Rear Admiral Casey's flagship, a salute
was fired. The Oregon then made a circle
around/' the Iowa and Philadelphia and
came to anchor off Steuart-street wharf..
- Her homeward bound pennant streamed
twice the" length of the ship astern." A
magnificent silk flag, the gift, of .the citi
zens of Nagasaki, Japan, floated over the
stt-rn and a silk Jack in the bow. Travel
stained, and looking' considerably the

OUR own Oregon returns to her
home port .with no blush for her
career since she passed out
through the Golden Gate and
started on her long world rec
ord-beating: trip around the
Horn to the scene of impending battle in
Cuban waters. -v ;
This voyage of 14,000 miles, made In six
ty-six days, reflected the highest credit
cn the men who designed her, on those
who built her and on the officers and men
who handled her. F*or speed and endur
ance ehe proved herself a genuine thor
oughbred and won the praises and plaud
its of the world, which was absolutely
emazed at her wonderful performance.
"The Bulldog of the American â¢ Navy"
was the title beetowed upon her for the
daring vigrjr and tenacity with which she
pursued the ships of Cervera's fleet at
Santiago on the ever memorable July 3,
3%t>8. Kor actual performance she stands
first among the warships of the world.
San Francisco, whose mechanics built
her. may well be proud of her; also our
neighbor State to the north, in whoso
honor she was named.
The Oregon with the Indiana and-Mas
sachusetts was built under the act of
Congress of 1890 authorizing the construc
tion of three first class battleships. Â¦ The
contracts for the two last \ named were
given to the Cramps of Philadelphia and
the contract for the Oregon to the Union
Iron Works of this city. Her keel was
laid In 1S91 and on October 26, 1S93, she
was put Into the water. When finished
she had a displacement of 11,000. tons, i
The contract speed was fifteen knots per
At ' every port touched ' at the Oregon
â¢was given an enthusiastic reception, but
still there is not a man aboard but that la
glad to be home again. v,
The battleship carries the crack base
ball team of the fleet While the ship was
lying In Hongkong every nine in the
China squadron r was" met, and beaten.
Finally one' morning a signal was sent to
the flagship, .asking her to pick a team
from the entire fleet and . flit â¢ it against
the Oreeon's team. This was done and
the Oregon boys were again victorious.
Of all the men -who left here on. the Ore
gon three years ago only two are still
aboard. They are Carpenter J. P. Tates
and Gunner Simon Jacobs. " Boatswain
Murphy, one of Hobson's heroes, who was
Injured, at Honolulu, is rapidly Improving
and Is now able to move about with the
aid of a walking stick. ,

INSURGENT LEADER - -
IS STTLIi DEFIANT
-The boat is submerged until her deck
is nearly awash, solely by filling her bal
last tank with water. Diving Is accom
plished by means of diving-rudders,. the
boat always having buoyancy, . so that
in. the event of a stoppage she will come
to the surface. > >-;â¢ ' Â¦ â¢
The Fulton is an improved model of
naval warfare, and is 63 feet 4 inches long
and 11 feet 9 inches beam. A section of
the boat shows circular formation and
her submerged displacement is 120 tons.
She has a 160 horsepower gasoline engine
for surface running and a 70 horsepower
electric motor- for driving her under
water. This power is obtained from stor
age batteries carried on board. She is
expected to make eight knots an hour" on
the surface and seven knots when sub
merged. Her armament ' will be five
Whitehead torpedoes, which, can be dis
charged either when the vessel is sub
merged or on the surface, with a speed
of about I twenty-two knots, an hour.
The only exposed part of the boat when
not submerged is the conning tower,
which Is made of Harveyized steel twen
ty-four inches in diameter and four inches
thick. In this conning tower there are
eight sights,, allowing observation in all
directions by the navigator. The crew
Â¦will -.consist of seven men. . . ;
t. Â¦

to a halt and was towed to an anchorage

the vessel was painted a dark red and the
upper part a light gray..

Participants in South
African Campaign
Honored.
LONDON, June 12.â London seldom has
a finer spectacle than was witnessed on
the Horse Guards parade to-day, when
King Edward presented medals to 3000
scldier participants in the South African
campaign. The great square was lined
with guards drawn from the various regi
ments. In the center of the ground was
a purple covered dais surmounted by an
Indian tent with silver corner-poles. In
the space between the dais and St. James
Park were drawn up 3000 officers and men
of the Guards, Household Cavalry and
City Imperial Volunteers, all of whom
had served in the campaign. The Admir
alt>\ Horse Guards and other official
buildings fronting- the parade were all
decorated with flags. From the house of
Joseph H. Choate. the United States Em
bassador, on Carlton House terrace, flew
the American flag. The Lord Â¦ Mayor,
Frank Green, attended in state and the
members of the special Moorish embassy
in picturesque costumes were interested
spectators^
Promptly at 11 o'clock King Edward,
the Queen, Princess Victoria and other
members of the royal family arrived and
took up places on the dais and the cere
mony began. The recipients of the med
als. In a. lone line, marched past the
King, received the decoration from his
Majesty, saluted and passed on. Lord
Roberts came first, followed by Lord Mil
ner. while behind them came crowds of
generals and lesser officers -whose names
have become familiar owing to the warâ
Buller, Ian Hamilton and a host of others.
Among the members of Lord Roberts'
South African staff who received the med
al was Captain the Duke of Marlboroughc
The procession as a whole was most in
congruous. The officers of- the Guards,
Lancers. Hussars and ! Highlanders, in
dazzling uniforms: groups of solemnly
garbed men in frock coats, do.ctors who
had served at the front and half a dozen
foreign attaches in uniform. Captain
Stephen L. N. Slocum, the United States
military attache, -was not present. He is
In St. Petersburg. There were also groups
of time-expired men in civilian clothing,
policemen and wounded soldiers limping
along In hospital clothes. .The ceremony
lasted nearly three hours. ' the -Queen
standing beside the King throughout. .

not require one cent's' worth of repair*.

REGARDING the advantage of a wireless telegraphy system between the Farallon Islands and San Francisco,
via Point Lobos, am pleased to say that I believe, if the system is practicable, its advantage to the shipping
int-^ests would more than justify the expense of construction and maintenance. It is one of the many recent,
â¢advances in electrical science that has practical value, and should be put into operation .wherever it^can be
used to the benefit of our commerce. . . . ' \-. -. ,
The Merchants' Exchange has its lookout station at Point Lobos at a high elevation! and as the.Faral
lone Islands are of sufficient height, the expense of construction would be minimum. The Merchants'. Ex
change has a telephone line, connecting the Point Lobos station with the exchange, so that Â¦ any . messages sent in
could be promptly transmitted to the proper address. . . V Â¦ â¢,' â¢ Â» i( _
I beUeve it to be within the province of this Government to construct such telegraphic , system . for the better
protection of our merchant marine. Also, it would be of advantage to vessels in . the Government -} service, â¢â¢. They
could communicate with or receive orders without the necessity of coming into the harbor. Vesselsjarrlving off the
oort in distress or requiring the services of a tug or other assistance, could, by signaling the. operator. on the islands,
communicate with the city. Vessels coming to this coast In ballast' with Instructions to get their orders off the har
bor (to come in or proceed to Portland or the Sound for their outward cargo) would be saved the inconvenience of
delays Such communication now is generally by ths pilot boats and necessitates considerable loss of time. > '.
New and valuable uses not now thought of, would develop through the ability to communicate between the Far
allones and this city by the installation of a telegraphic system, otherwise. -:Tour8^pectfully. . - ... ;
i,;.;^.V. G. W. McNEAR, President Merchants ; Exchange.

LEADING MEN OF THE COAST NOW URGING
ESTABLISHMENT OF MARCONI'S WIRELESS
TELEGRAPHY AT HEADS AND FARALLONES
CAMFORXIAXS have for many years held
the conceit that San Franclico occupied
one of the moot Important small spot*
upon the face of the globe. Time in
about to prove that they were not very
far wroDR. The rent of the world Â¦ has
discovered our Importance and is pre
pared to admit that ire hold possession of a
most important stopping; place on the world's
principal highway. - Foreigners admit it and
onr national legislators nre waking np to it.
We need a fair share of Uncle Sam's spending:
money and we -want Congress to realize onr
needs. We need every aid possible in con
nection â¢with onr great ' harbor. We should
long ago have had a Government telegraph to
the Farallones. Such a \ natural outpost in
front of a great harbor should be pat in. dt
rect communication with the main land. The
ships and the travelers of the world require it
and the nation should provide it. The -wire
less telegraphy has apparently come' to stay
and we should demand of the Government that
one of the first applications of its use should
be bet-ween the Farallones and the main land.
Suppose we learn a little from' our college
students. Suppose we select a yell leader and
stand . by him. Let all the commercial inter
ests unite, let the press unite, let the Con
gressional delegation unite, let us all .act to
gether and shout together, "We want wireless
telegraphy to the Farallones," and doubtless
we will get it. , j FRAXK J. SYJIMES,
President of- Merchants' Association.

NEW YORK, June M2.â The World has
this from London: The -British Govern
ment has decided to levy $250,000,000 on
the Transvaal gold mines, the World cor
respondent learns from a sure' source, to
half pay the cost of the war ;
Sir David Barbour, formerly Finance
Minister for India, who was commis
sioned to report upon the taxable . ca
pacity of these mines, advised Colonial
Secretary Chamberlain to levy $175,000,000,
but Mr. Chamberlain, after consultation
with Lord Milner, increased the maxi
mum to the amount stated. Barbour's
report is awaited here with the keenest
interest in political and financial circles.
This recommendation, when it becomes
known here, is certain to cause con
sternation among Kaffir stockholders,
who fancied that the idea of taxing the
mines, which are mostly owned in Eng
land, had been abandoned.' As these very
gold mines: were the real underlying
cause of the. war, many have maintained
that, their British owners should be made
to bear -a large share, at- least, of its
heavy burden. .

SHREVEPORT. La., June 12.â John G.
Foster was killed on the Foster plantation
five miles west of 'this city on the Vicks
burg, Shreveport and Pacific Railway this
morning by. a- man named Prince Ed
wards. . There had been trouble between
the overseers and negroes, and Foster left
the plantation store to go to the scene
of the trouble. Twelve or fifteen negroes
had. gathered in a cabin, and as Foster
approached one of .those in the. cabin fired,
mortally wounding him. The negroes scat
tered but several bands of officers have
succeeded in capturing all but Edwards,
who did the shooting.

REFERRING to -the establishment of â¢ a wireless telegraph" system -!
from the Farallon Islands for the purpose' of advising our Chamber
of Commerce and, the shipping community of the approach of vessels
to our coast. I would say that before'leaving Washington I had sev- "
eral interviews with Professor Willis L. Moore, Chief of the Weather
Bureau in Washington, in relation to- the wireless telegraph. He,
being both a progressive and a scientific man, has been investigating
the practicability of the use of the wireless telegraph to be used by vessels on
the Pacific Coast to advise "us in relation to the cold waves and the condition
of the weather on -the coast of the States of Washington, Oregon and Cali
fornia. The result of his investigations when. I left Â¦ Washington had not
satisfactorily demonstrated to his mind the practicability of asking the Gov- '
ernment for an appropriation, but he hoped' to gather sufficient data to en
able him to make a. favorable repoTt when Congress convenes next December.
Since I have been in Congress I have endeavored to secure. ah appropria
tion for the construction of a cable line from' Point Reyes to the Farallon
Islands, as that seemed to be the most advantageous point on the coast. If
it is not deemed practicable to use the wireless telegraph, then I am. . very"
hopeful that we will be able to secure an appropriation at tho coming session
of Congress this winter for a cable from San Francisco or Point Reyes to the
Farallon Islands. , ' . Â¦ 'Â¦
The experiment or trial now being made by the New York Herald, estab
lishing a wireless telegraph service between Nantucket lightship and the
headland on the island, will demonstrate the practicability of such a service.
.There is no question but what it will come In the near future," buf I think it j
the part of wisdom to wait and profit by the experience of those who are
scientifically investigating the whole subject. GEORGE -C.PERKINS,
United States Senator' from California.
CALIFORNIA SENATOR
FORESEES SUCCESS
THE establishment. by the Xew York Herald of a wireless telegraph
service between Nantucket lightship and a head land on the island
furnishes a suggestion for our own coast, which should be promptly
acted upon, to wit: The establishment of a similar service between
the Farallones and San Francisco. No difficulties present themselves
in establishing and successfully operating such a service here which
have not been met and overcome there. The distance between the
operating points is not . so great here as there, and the conditions of eleva
tion, etc.. are all in our favor. '/
It is not necessary to enumerate here the advantages and benefits to ac
crue from the establishment of such a service. Many of these immediately sug
gest themselves coincidently with mention of it; and, with further thought,
others present themselves, among which will prominently appear the possi
bility of preventing by its aid such terrible disasters as that of the Rio, and
others which have preceded it, at least of diminishing the loss of life in case
of such accidents â the approach of -vessels being announced long In advance,
and their arrival looked for. â¢ ' Â¦ â¢
The subject is one to which my attention has previoftsly been called. Just
before I left Washington last March it formed the subject of a very Inter
esting interview with a gentleman who had given it much intelligent thought
and careful study, with, as it seemed to me at the time, entirely practical
conclusions.
The great desirability of establishing the service being admitted, consid
eration of the means and the successive steps for its speedy realization are
next in order. These> shall at once command my earnest attention, as they
undoubtedly/ will also that of my colleagues, and the result of our co-ope
rative action should greatly aid In securing a consummation in the short
est possible time. .Â» V. H. METCALF,
Representative Third Congressional District.

i . S . president of the Manufacturers' and- Producers' Association, I

ter for national concern and pride. "We are facing the Orient, and
that fact alone should be a sufficient incentive for the Government
to take such action as may be necessary Mn the premises. The wonderful
use of electricity in wireless telegraphy has made possible in the near fu
ture that which would *btherwise have been remote. I must applaud thÂ©
enterprise and public spirit of The Call in malting a strong effort* to have
wireless telegraphy applied to the problem of communication for the bene
fit of our shipping, commercial and other material interests. The advan
tages that would come to us from having certain and quick means of hear
ing from our ships and the ships of other nations coming within sighting dis
tance'of the Farallones are too many to be easily enumerated. The estab
lishment of 'stations for the transmission of information between Nan
tucket lightship and the mainland indicates that unless we are equipped
with facilities we _ shall be distanced in very important particulars by the
enterprising Eastern people.

ciation, which was organized for the purpose of aiding in the advancement
of California in every possible way. At our front door rolls the broad Pa
cific Ocean, Inviting enterprise and promising us wealth, prosperity and fu
ture development in large measure. It only remains for us to make the most
of Â¦ our natural advantages to become vastly prosperous.. And In our "ad
vance we shall prove to be of inestimable service to the whole- country.

indorse the proposition to have wireless telegraphy established ' between the
Farallones snd San Francisco, and hope that success may attend the very
laudable and public spirited efforts of The Call in this direction. .

! ST. PETERSBURG, .June 12.â A small
notice published In the papers here chron
icles Â¦ the result of one of the greatest
pieces of commercial folly on the part of
the. United States which.it is possible to
imagine. That notice tells of an order
of the Minister of Finance raising the tax
on several articles of -American produc
tion, including bicycles, for example, from
20 to 30 per cent, which means .virtually
that they are excluded.
Eighteen millions of dollars' worth of
American trade per annum is for the same
reason brought to an end.
And this is all done upon a pure tech
nicality â upon which most people here be
lieve to be a misunderstanding. r It is all
over the matter of Russian sugar and it
is what is known in trade as "bounty
fed."
Mr. Holloway, United States Consul
here, took upon himself the responsibility
of reporting to the Treasury Department
that Russian exported sugar was indirect
ly "bounty fed." His decision is generally
controverted. In the mean time harm has
been done.
To put the matter simply so that all can
understand it, the case- is this: Russia
guarantees to take from* her sugar pro
ducers so much sugar at a. certain sum,
upon which she places an internal tax.
At the same time she taxes all the sugar
produced. When the manufacturers have
a surplus for exportation that internal tax
is refunded to them. "SK
How such a condition can possibly be
tortured into a bounty is what the people
here cannot understand, bounty being a
sum Daid to stimulate production. A
bounty is paid by Germany to some manu
facturers. For so much exported so much
cash is given per ton to the exporters.
Here the internal revenue tax is merely,
remitted. . .

lishing Company.

Dr. Benjamin F. de Costa delivered his
second lecture of the Catholic Truth So
ciety lecture course last evening before a
large and Interested audience In -Metro
politan Temple, the subject being- "The
VaHcan Archives and the. Papal Bulls Re
lating to Pre-Columbian America." ' . ,
Frank J. Kierce", vice president of the
Catholic Truth Society, introduced the
speaker of the evening.
Dr. de Costa in the course of his lec
ture affirmed that it was an incontrovert
ible fact .that European civilization was
represented in the Western world in pre-
Columbian" times; that this civilization
was the exclusive product of the Catholic
church, which, und^er â¢ a succession ; of
bishops, was maintained for many genera
tions in Greenland, where there was a
cathedral, \ the ruins of which remain to
day, besides sixteen parish churches. Â»
A similar statement was made respect
ing the representation of the Catholio
church on the mainland of America by
both the Irish and Scandinavians and the
speaker declared that New England was
reached by Bishop Eric and other Catholic
missionaries. - ,
The speaker said also that it was highly
probable that the system of collecting
tithes in Greenland for the Crusades was
employed in New England. He argued
further that the supremacy of the Pope
was reedgnized in America in pre-Colum
bian times, as It Is to-day.
Greenland was discovered In 983 by Eric
the Red, who had been banished from Ice
land for manslaughter, the 'speaker said.
Eric returned to -Iceland, announced his
discovery and brought back with him to
the new - country several priests. The
country was rapidly settled and several
excursions were made from Greenland to
the coast of New England. The first pil
grimage from America was made by' an
Icelandic nun, who went from New Eng
land to Rome. . .
"All the records of the Holy See prior
to the year 1206 A. D. have disappeared,"
said the speaker. "The archives of the
Vatican are absolutely free for the in
spection of the public. The Vatican is no
conspiracy against history; it has nothing
to conceal. There is nothing in the secret
archives of which the Catholic church
need be afraid or ashamed. They are be
ing consulted daily by students and phil
osophers. If the archives of Rome had
remained intact they, would have shown
that communication with America had
been kept up through Greenland and Ice
land."
Speaking of the discovery of Iceland and
Greenland, Dr. de Costa said that it was
being proved by recent researches that
the Irish discovered Iceland and Green
land before the Norsemen did.
Â¦, "There is hardly a nook or a corner in
the United States that is not now being
visited â¢ by some Irish priest,"- said the
speaker.

On Tuesday evening there will be a spe
cial session of Hartnagle Chapter, the
highest degree of Druidism, for the pur
pose of receiving into it a class of eli
gibles; on Wednesday afternoon there
will be a. meeting of the Past Arches' As
sociation to admit a number of the repre
sentatives to the Grand" Grove, and on
that evening there will be a reception in
Odd Fellows' Hall to the grand officers
and the representatives to the Grand
Grove and the Grand Circle. A ball will
follow the reception.
Grand Grove of California,
I United Ancient Order of Druids,
I will meet in annual session in the
LL Druidic Temple on Sutter street
next Tuesday. This jurisdiction
has now eighty-six groves as against sev
enty-nine at the opening of the previous
session. Eight new groves were instituted
during the year and one surrendered Its
charter. ; The membership at this time is
nearly 6000. During the term of the pres
ent noble grand arch, Henry Lange,
there were 1200 initiations and the rein
statement of 200 suspended members,
which Is equal to 1400 new members. Aa
an offset there was the loss of one grove"
with 70 members and the suspension of 460
members on account of neglect to pay
dues, leaving a net gain of 870.
aTS Ine^ e Consul General Ho Tow has
dec ared diplomatic war against Customs
< ollector Stratton because of a difference
Â«f opinion between them respecting the
â¢J-t Â° ShIh Ch ' n> the reform leader.
The Collector has expressed the opinion
for publication that, under the terms of
the exclusion laws, he is not bound to
notice the fact that Su is or Is not a re
former or a revolutionist, so longr as he
holds thr certificate required by law for
h!s admissicn as .-Â» member of a non-ex
i-Iutfed oJufe.
Ho Yow thinks differently. He has ex
cused the Collector of discriminating, and
lias filed a formal complaint with the Sec
retary of the Treasury against the Col
lector.
fact is," said Ho Yow yesterday,
"that the section 6 certificate presented
by Su Shih Chin was obtained by fraud
from the Chinese Government, and sec
ondly, it is irregular on its face. Real
merchants have been denied a landing
here because their papers have been Ir
regular in form, and they will have a
right to kick if the Collector passes over
the matter of the irregularity in the form
of Su's certificate."
The following protest was telegraphed
last Tuesday evening by Ho Yow to the
Secretary of the Treasury:
IMPERIAL. CHINESE CONSULATE.
SAX FRANCISCO, June 11. 1901.
Hon. Secretary of Treasury, Washington, D.
C: Tour telegram received. Collector decided
or recommended case of Su Shih Chin with
out calling or hearing our evidence, although
I hed filed charges with him & few days pre
vious, but will do eo now. We allege his sec
tion 6 papers are obtained by fraud, besides
being irregular in form. Chinese Minister at
Washington could be communicated with for
further evidence, as he could ascertain the
genuineness of the papers from our Govern
ment or the Customs Taotal at Shanghai, who
issued these papers to Su Shih Chin. I respect
fully submit that you request the Collector
to forward the criminal certificates in dispute
with all others. Please defer decision until
facts or evidence arrive from China. Respect
fully. HO YOW.
Imperial Chinese Consul General.
Yesterday morning- the following note
was handed to the Collector by the Chi
nese Consul General:
IMPERIAL, CHINESE CONSULATE.
SAX FRANCISCO. June 12. 1901,
Hon. F. S. Stratton. Collector of Customs â
Dear Sir: Your favor of yesterday's date vas
duly received about 4 p. m.. notifying me of
your desire to accord me a hearing in the case
of Su Shih Chin. In it you also stated your
impression that I only withdrew our guaranty
â¢â¢as to the ofScial character of Su Shih Chin,"
and merely desired his case to follow the or
oir.ary procedure where applicants present
themselves for landing."
It ie true to a certain extent that I inti
mated this, but even if it were so my letter
to you about this man sets down many charges,
besides stating he is not a bona fide official.
In examining into his case as ordinary ap
plicants for landing I had hoped in fairness
to all concerned that you would look for evi
dence material to this case and in the quarter
in which you are aware you could obtain and
hear both or all sides of the case before you
report or recommend the case to the Treasury
Department. Many real Chinese merchants
coming under the same section 6 papers have
been denied a landing for slighter causes be
fore. But as you have an independent au
thority and power to regulate and decide all
ordinary Chinese cases it would be discourteous
for me or any outsider to comment or inter
fere with your procedure, but since you have
mentioned the matter in your kind favor of
. yesterday I respectfully take the liberty of
Ttuchtnc tipen it. .- â
Too are kind enough in the same note to
. accord me the privilege of fixing the day and
hour for a hearing, to which I beg the liberty
of naming Thursday at 2:30 p. m., which I
tiape will be agreeable to you. Respectfully,
HO YOW.
Imperial Chinese Consul General.
In response to the diplomatic note the
Collector fixed to-day at 2:30 p. m. as the
time for the examination of Su's papers.
"If his papers are regular," said the j
Collector yesterday, "I can see no reason
Â¦why he should not be allowed to land. I
cannot go into' the question as to whether
he is a reformer or not. I shall then
transmit the evidence to Washington, as
the Secretary of the Treasury has ex
pressed a desire to pass upon this case
himself." ' -
Ho Yow insists that Su is an outlaw
and should not be admitted on any papers
purporting to have been issued or in
dorsed by the Chinese Government.

MRS. NATION'S LAST CHANCE.
Government . cannot consider "extremes alone, yet
it, is Mrs. Nation's idea tHat" only'' the" abnormal. is
normal.
But hers is a mistaken theory. It is not only well
but necessary to base j conclusions tip'ori the average
;ind nct-upon the extreme. of human experience. All
arc not happy," and- all are 'not unhappy. All women
have not â¢ witnessed the alcoholic extinction of one
husband.' and perhaps been physically disciplined! by,
another, and all grandmothers have ,not had frivolous
grandsons, whose sole gift was in making a cocktail
move itself aright and show its ; color. in the cup.
If all lives had touched and' sliared. an equal series
of abnormalities she would-be everywhere welcome
as an cxpcrt.in morbid experiences. Her government
will naturally, be based upon thcprinciple that all peo
ple have lived and endured as she has. That'is the
theory of her /crusade. * - The ..Jate ;Immanuel Kant
would say that what lx> her is posteriori she assumes
to be a priori to all others.
In' Bryan's State a town -of 1200 inhabitants at the
tprih'g'election nominated and elected a ticket pledged
to import. Mrs. Nation and consult her and follow, her
advjee in everything pertaining to the government of
the municipality. The officers so chosen have organ
ized and notified the lady to come, and stand ad- 1
journe4 .trtitil she appears, -with hatchet in her hand
and blood in her cyi. The officers explain to their,
constituents that they will not move a wheel until
they get her advice. As her -knowledge of laws and
constitutions has not been shown to exceed that of*
Moses, Solon, Justinian, Honorius and other prac
titioners^-ancient and modern, it is not probable that
she will be subject to the limitations which such
knowledge implies in putting in practice this novel
scheme of government. It will be, in fact, a
Carnation set up within the State of Nebraska and
exceptional to all the statutory regulations governing
municipalities. What she says goes, and that's the end
of it. It- will-be' interesting to >watch the smasher as
she enters upon and rules her new estate. Perhaps
her somewhat varied and unfortunate personal expe
rience^ will be the guide of her new jurisdiction. Her
first husband floated hence on the flowing bowl, and it
is alleged that her second applied physical discipline
to her to such an extent that Texas refused to con
tain him; and her grandson r mixes drinks in a Chicago
joint/. ' Â¦";â¢ \- : , . ... .. : . ' .". .,; . Â¦â¢ Â¦;'-â¢ Â¦'' Â¦
While she bandied; words . with Justices of' "the
Peace or browbeat Judges she amused the multitude.
While, she cheerfully consigned smokers and drinkers
to ircIUshe awestruck the many who -still believe in the
mysterious, and fear the power of prophets, seers and
rcvclators. ' But all that time she had no real dis
ciplesj and. even Mr. .Nation growled about the ex
pense arid pointed to his buttonless trousers and un
washed Shirts. But all this is changed. The prophet
ess is not without honor, though it comes from out
side her own cpuntry. The stone fined by a Kansas
court has been laid' for a corner in Nebraska.
While Mrs. Nation went around playing a resound
ing solo with her hatchet she was an object of interest
or of fear, according to the, point of view.
But the novel and extraordinary in moral and po
litical* reform never goes aright until it secures dis
ciples!
SINXE Mrs.. Nation was actually fined by a court,
and Mr. Nation fled from the conjugal presence
to live with his children, there has been some
danger of that energetic lady and her hatchet passing
from public view. \ . *. * .

MANCHURIA AN0MTS CTRADE:
WOMEN FOR SOUTH AFRICA.
EVER since Chamberlain niade his famous
speech suggesting an extensive emigration of
British women to South Afriea as soon as the
war is over there has been an organized effort to edu
cate women for the work likely to be required of them
should they decide upen the venture. The instruction
has been undertaken by* the United British Women's
Emigration Society, and is being carried out with en
ergy. The number of women who have applied for.
instruction is large, and most of them arc said to be of
a high order of ability, .for the society* discourages
Avoinen of the servant class owing to the.fact that there
is an. abundance of labor of that kind already in the
country. Â¦ ..â.".. â â¢
The secretary of the society in explaining the work
to the London Chronicle, said recently: ,"We. welcome
the time which must necessarily elapse before the
country will have settled down to industry, for we do
not want to send out a number of raw, untrained
women. As:it is, we are enabled to encourage them to
train for definite occupations. Just now, by means of
a pamphlet written by. Lady Knightley of JFawsley we
are appealing for. the active :co-operatidn \ of - : ladies
throughout .the; country in enabling intending emi
grants to obtain instruction in' various departments of
practical .life, such as" cooking, , dairying, poultry
keeping, "bread-making, laundry "work, needlework,

AFTER her glorious voyage around the Horn,
her share in the victory at Santiago and her
service in the Orient, the peerless Oregon has
returned to San Francisco to' rest for a time in the
bay that first received her. It is right and fitting that
her return; was greeted- by ' a distinguished welcome,
for slier has % reflected .Â£<?Â£ much honor upon the Â£ort
where" sTiV was" built "that she deserves honors in re
turn.
In the navies of the world many a battleship floats
upon the waters, but of them all the Oregon is at this
time supreme." It is undeniable that she is the most
famous warship upon the, seas. Her voyage around the
Horn was^a magnificent triumph of speed and en
durance. No other vessel of her class has had her
machinery and her hull so continuously and so , tre
mendously tested, or has borne the test with such re
markable success. She made a voyage which in. itself
amazed the naval experts of the world, and at the end
of it she arrived xipon the scene of war not only fit
to enter battle but did actually enter battle and stand
pre-eminent in the conflict.
â¢ The oldest and the greatest ship-building ports in
the world would be proud of constructing such a
vessel as the. Oregon. It is therefore but,natural that
San Franciscp should feel a joy in her matchless feats
and her peerless position among .the' warships of the
time.. She attests the skill and fidelity of our mechan
ics and ship-builders not less than the", courage and
seamanship of her commander and her; crew. She
was built stanch and strong in every part of her com
plex mechanism, a fit upholder of the flag of the re
public, and it is with a patriotic satisfaction as well
as a civic pride we welcome her home again.

Habits of Thought and Study.
I think that the school hours of our life
in cities axe too many for boys and girls
who go to school forty-four weeks in the
year.' In the better system of the older
country districts, where a school is closed
after, thirteen weeks* . winter study, and
not opened again until after a long period
of work on the farm or in the household,
why, there is no harm in seven or even
eight hours a day for study, while the
scholars ar-i at school. . - â <*'
A certain dawdling habit comes In. with
the long scaool session which is bad all
around. And many a boy and girl has to
break up this habit, and turn over a new
leaf when they form the habit of reading,
writing and thought upon which they arc
to go through life. _. .
In the first place, let nobody bo gloomy
because he has not " more .than two or
three hours for study in every day. I do
not say that' a man needs. no more, If he
be working out some of the great prob
lems or enterprises. If a. man were de
signing a new locomotive, -or if he were
contriving a new J pattern for a dollar
watch, or devising a new light like Edi
son's, he would give much more, than
three hours a day for stiff brain work â
for study.
But this does not mean that* a man
needs .more than two or three hours a
day, for book study. "Sunday will come
in, .as Andrew Fairservice says, and that
will give him more. In the Chautauqua
courses, which have proved very well de
vised for the average American working
man, we find, that eight closely applied
reading hours in a week will meet our re
quisitions, though we ask for more. I
know thai almost anybody, however
much occupied, can get fourteen .reading
hours every week, and for people who
come to me for advice I order that, as'if
I were a doctor with a prescription blank,
and Rx at the top of it.
Hours of Reading and Study.
As to the "hours of reading or careful
study, man or woman must take it when
they can. You are always safe, however.
If you can leave your bed ' so early aa to
get an hour for reading or writing or
study before you go to your day'a regu
lar work. Only you must take some food
before you begin to work your brain.
You nre on ticklish ground again If you
work the bruin hard in the houra before
sleep. This haa been raid already and. the
reason given.
Hut simple reading,' for the acquisition
of knowledge or for pleasure, does not
work or tire the brain. Heading aloud In
the evening Is an excellent habit In the
family for the lust hour or hours of a
day.
It is all very well to be an all-round
mnn or wom.ui, and to keep, in a way,
even with what the world la doing In its
larger Interests. But all the same, who
ever takes life seriously will be wise if he
determines that he will keep himself well

wnich Interests him. Is It flowers, Is It
the care of bees, is it trees, is it grass, Is
It local history, is it history of the na
tion, is It the tariff, la it municipal light
ing? 1 do not know. You know what you
like, what interests you. You know what
will be of use In your daily occupation.
You know how you can help the neigh
bors and -make. the town a better town.
If you know that you know what is your
special line of reading, or, if you .please,
of study. And my advice is that you
form the habit of keeping up your read
ing in that direction.

Any one 'who knows a bicycle ought to
know enough of the machine to keep it in
order. He" need not be a machinist, but

to ell, and how much oil to use.
. Now the same thing is true of mind and

sciatic nerve divides. But he is the mas

Thus the man succeeds in whatever Is in
hand, because he brings to bear infinite
power.

Union of Body, Mind and Soul.
I havyqbeen asked to write a few words,
not too many, ..on regular habits in the
training of life, as they look to an old man
who never had to go to bed in the day
time and who never "enjoyed ill^health."
-Body, mind and soul all -belong togeth
er; and we are fools if we try to separate
them. "We must take care of them to
gether. We must train them together. We
do not want first-rate gymnasts or ath
letes, unless they are first-rate men; we
do not want expert calculators or bril
liant authors unless they are first-rate
men; and we. have no use for the monk
of the middle ages who spent day and
night 'in prayer and meditation, with a
body which has no nerve or muscle or go
and a mind which was addled and vague
in its spasmodic action. This man, with
his cosseting of what he called his soul,
was not a first-rate man, nor a second
rate-mnn. It is only by a stretch of,lan
guage that. he is called a man at all.

.People woo sleep: they call sleep fickle

Is not so if the sleep has not been sound

Every one will find out how much -sleep

LISTING- THE TOWN.
B7Y the activity in this city, of a lady engaged in
- getting up an entertainment for the benefit of
...the University Mound- OW Ladies' Home
there ha^been introduced to San Francisco an indus
try which in the East is known as "listing the town.'^
The business is simple and . easily understood." The
promoter of the scheme arranges with some well estab
lished charity for" the use of its name in getting up'an
entertainment and then proceeds to list the town and
sell tickets right and left. The charity receives a lump
sum or a percentage en the sales of seats, , or both,
and the promoter gets whatever there is in excess of
those amounts. As the American people are ever
liberal in support of local charities, it will be perceived
that the business of working up such entertainments
can readily be made profitable.
Charity by. percentage may be acceptable in the
East, but it is not needed here, and our charitable as
sociations should not lend their names to the pro
moters of such schemes. If there were no other ob
jection to the method it would be. objectionable from
the very fact that by lending their names and giving
indorsement to such promoters the . local charities
would encourage the practice," and- before long evils
would result. Business men and others who have
been ready to * support our home institutions would
not know to whom they were called on to give money
or for what they were giving.it. There would be no
ready way of determining. a meritorious enterprise of
that kind from one promoted solely to make money
for the promoter, and as a consequence .there would
soon be a lack of public confidence which would re
sct to the injury of home charities. .
; The main objection to percentage charity is that
even when honorably conducted" it means that a por
tion of the money that should go to the home institu
tion will pass to another beneficiary. The local char
ity receives only a percentage of the income. Our
business men would thus be called upon to sustain
not only the home charities but to pay for the privi
lege of- doing it. There would be an added burden
upon the communityâ a burden uncalled for, unneces
sary and unjustifiable.
Had the merchants and others of the city who are
relied upon to contribute to every form of charitable
work been illiberal or backward in their. contributions,
perhaps it would be advisable to call in a professional
promoter to raise funds, but no such condition ex
ists. Our people have been generous and prompt in
responding to every rightful appeal made to them in
such cases. No community sustains in proportion to
its population a more abundant charity by voluntary
contribution than does San Francisco.. The.calls.upon
our merchants are many, and it is not fair to add to
them by asking them to purchase tickets for percent
age entertainments. There are some-forms of activity
developed in the East of which we can well afford
to allow our Eastern friends to enjoy the monopoly,
and this is one of them.-

THE attention of -the legal and the
laity has been fixed upon the Supreme^Court
arid engrossed in its decision of. the island cases
-tOjSUch.an extent that contemporary decisions of vast
importance are^not considered. One of them; of far
reaching influence, has just been rendered" by 'Hon.
Smith McPherson, Judge of. the United States Dis
trict Court for the Southern District of Iowa. -It re
verses all the courts in a matter that has been the
subject of a vast mass of litigation.
Life insurance policies contain a suicide clause,
providing that if the policy holder take his own life,
sane or insane, the insurance contract *is vitiated' and
the policy is void.
life : insurance contract peculiarly Â¦affects .third
parties. The second party to such a contract, the per
son insured, cannot get any value received out of a
life policy that runs to the surviving ""beneficiaries.
Their interests, then, are affected by the form of con
tract made by the policy holder, and it is their interest
with which the courts deal in life insurance .litigation.
A contract of life Insurance- containing the suicide
clause was litigated before Judge -McPherson and he
decided the clause void, 1 because a sane man cannot
make a contract that. shall bind an insane man; while
sane he cannot bind himself not* to do certain things
if he become insane and malic that Jhe essence of a
contract. ' ' " ,
The insurance contract was held valid and the policy
payable to its beneficiaries. We think that the equity
of this decision will be seen a^t once and that insur
ance companies will abandon that clause in their con
tracts. â¢ . . â¢
Illuminated by. Judge McPherson's decision it is
plainly seen that an insurance contract might as well
provide that it is to be void if the insured die of any
other disease besides mental disorder. Mental and
physical diseases may come against all human precau
tion, and to bind a man not to die of insanity through
suicide is like binding him not to die of any other
disease. In the nature of things such an, undertaking
is impossible, and such a contract is against good
conscience and public policy.
The Judge has risen above technicality, disregarded
stare decisis, precedent and settled rules, to rise into
the higher atmosphere of legal principles and bring
down therefrom an application of the science that is
as novel as it is necessary to the doing of justice.
It will be seen in the future that the application of
this principle will affect the contractual relation in
other directions, and wherever carried and properly
applied it will be found. serving the ends of pure jus-
tice. ~. ..
"Judge McPherson is a graduate of the law depart
ment of the Iowa Uiyversity, has served as Attorney
General of the State for"two terms and for three terms
in Congress, and was appointed to the bench by
President McKinley.. â¢'
It is probable that) the genius shown in this deci
sion and the fame of it in the profession will mark
him for still higher judicial preferment.

TO SAIL..
Attorneys Get Big Fee.
Thomas D. Rlordan and T. Z. Blakeman,
attorneys for. William Alvord,. Edwin R.
L*ande and I.' M. Friedberg, executors 'of
the will of the late Julius Friedman, were
The melting snow of the mountains is
booming the' far-famed falls; the trees
have put on their summer foliage, and
from the high Sierras to the Merced River
in Its picturesque windings through the
valley, the views are magnificent. While
the month of June is a delightful time of
the year In the mountains. It is a hot and
generally dusty one In the plains and
foothills. The choice of a route to the
valley Is therefore worthy of careful con
sideration.. The Big Oak Flat Stage Route
is the most direct to the valley and is
singularly free from dust. The scenery
along the road from the first station at
the .summit of Priest's Hill- to the very
entrance to the, valley cannot be sur
passed. The -travel By this favored route
has increased to such an extent that the
stage company have been obliged to .vast
ly increase their transportation stock and
equipments. Every one who has traveled
over the Bis Oak Flat Route speaks in
highest terms of : tbeir treatment on the
road and at the stopping stations. Leav
ing San Francisco at 8:30 a. m., the tour
ist reaches s the Yo^emite Valley at 5 p. m.
of the following day, the intervening
night being comfortably spent at Priest's,
where the best of everything is provided
at very moderate charges. By. this route
no 'extras for' sleeping-car berths. You
go through by daylight. I and after a re
freshing sleep the first night at the hotel
you are prepared to enjoy the charming
scenery around from start to finish. . Tha
entire length of the stage route is less
than 60 milesâ the first ten of which is
made in the cool of the -late afternoon of
the first day. The fare by the Big Oak
Flat Route is lower than that of any
other route. V .

terday asking for a transfer from that de

In her petition for a: transfer Mrs.
Craven that during the hearing of
the petition for the distribution of the per

the court will be called upon to determine
the claim of the petitioner that she is the
widow of the deceased. She is entitled to
a ' transfer - of the' case, she says, on ac

cision heretofore filed in the matter of her
application for a ' family allowance, in
which Judge Troutt'held that the law and
the facts"presented during the hearing of,
the case demonstrated to his satisfaction
that the claims of the petitioner were not
based on facts.

until this morning, at which time It will
be argued. One of Mrs. Craven's attor
neys stated yesterday that Mrs. Craven
was financially able to carry on the flght

eventually accomplish her purpose. 1 **

NEWS OF THE OCEAN.
The Mail Company's overdue steamship
City of Kara arrived from Panama late
Tuesday night. She was delayed by head
winds. The Acapulco. now being cleaned
and painted on the Union Iron Works dry
dock will go out on the Para's run.
The British tramp steamer Condor is
completing her load pf lumber and rail
road ties for Guayaquil.
The Pacific Coast Steamship Company
has put the Cottage City on the Alaskan
excursion run in place of the Spokane.
The latter vessel, built especially for the
trade, cannot be completed owing- to the
strike. The City of Topeka and the AI-
Ki will also run during the season on
the Alaskan route.
The Â¦ battleship Wisconsin is expected
from Puget Sound to-morrow or Satur
day. On her arrival, San Francisco, for
the first time in its history, will have
three first-class battleships in port.
Water Front Notes.
The ship Governor Robie, fifty-seven
days from Newcastle. N. S. W., arrived
at Honolulu on June 2, leaking badly.
The schooner Laura Pike sailed from
Honolulu on May 26 for Eureka in ballast.
During heavy weather she sprang a leak
and had to put back.
Two Vessels Leaking.
The ex-transport Arab will be ready for
sea again soon.- She left here for lla
rila with a cargo of horses, but broke
down soon after leaving port and had to
put back. An examination showed that
nearly all her furnaces were down, and
the owners haci to send East for new
ones. When the furnaces arrived the ma
chinists' strike was on and the steamship
was tied up again. Captain Nelson, one
of the owners, has been negotiating with
the strikers ever since, and^ finall}- per
euaded a gang of them to^go to work at
their own figures yesterday. It will not
take long to get the furnaces in, and then
the Arab will get away from San Fran
cisco as Quickly as possible.
In spite of the strike, work is progres
sing slowly on. the Alaskan. Spokane,
Alameda and Sonoma, while the masts
have been removed from the Otto Gilde
meister and the vessel is being got into
shape again.
Repairs on the Arab.
The officers of the General Erisbie are:
captain. George Wheeler; pilot. Clarence
Fulton; chief engineer, James O'Xeill;
first assistant engineer, H. James; pur
ser, C. X. Hatch, and chief steward.tE.
M. Keith. Mr. Keith was with Captain
Hatch on the Sound fifteen years ago.
The Frisbie is owned by Hatch Bros.,
owners of the Monticello. The latter
boat is to be overhauled and will run to
Vallejo and on excursions in conjunction
with the General Frisbie.
The General Frisbie Is named after the
founder of Vailejo, who, years ago,
named the town alter his father-in-law,
General Vallejo. She is 170 feet over all,
26 feet beam, 13 feet deep, and Is licensed
to carry 450 passengers. On occasion,
however, a special license can be pro
cured to carry twice that number of peo
ple and then the steamship would not be
crowded. Everything that can possibly
conduce to the comfort of the passengers
has been provided, and the Frisbie is
already a favorite with Vallejo people.
She can make the run in an hour and "a
half, and with wind and tide against her
under the two hours.
The hull of the new steamship was built
at New Whatcom in the yards of the Bel
lingham Bay Improvement Company.
When completed she was loaded with
lumber and towed to San Franoisco. Her
engines and boilers were built and in
stalled by the United Engineering Works
and the Keystone Boiler Works, while
the electric light plant was put In by
Herzog & Dahl. The engines are of the
torpedo boat pattern and 1000 indicated
horsepower.
The handsome new steamship General
Frisbie had her owner's trial yesterday.
She was built for the Vallejo trade, and
was expected to make fifteen knots an
hour. On the run to Vallejo and back
yesterday she averaged* sixteen and one
half knots, and Captain Hatch says that
when the engines are" in perfect working
order she will do better than seventeen
knots.

Steamship. #

LAWSON'S BOAT
TO BE ALTERED
Independence Completes
First Series of Sail ; :
Trials, â¢ V :
Â¦ ' ~- â â¢ â - /
Mast Will Be Shortened and
Her Spread of Canvas
Reduced.
BOSTON, June 12.â The first of the se
ries of trials of the yacht Independence
was finished to-day, and trie big ninety
footer will not go out again until some
changes have been made to correct a few
slight defects, brought out during the five
days of: sailing. These changes will be a
shortening of the mast, a reduction of the
sailarea due to the tenderness shown by
the boat while on the wind and the sub
stitution of a stern-post rudder for the
balance affair/which has not proved sat
isfactory. The mast will be shortened ten
feet, four feet being taken off the
mainmast and six feet from Â¦ the foot
of the topmast. The reductions in the
sail area will come through the cutting
off of two feet from the top of the main
sail and some slight changes in the head
sails, or a tbtal of some 200 square feet.
This reduction will lighten the top
hamper and stiffen the yacht without ad
ditional ballast.
The Independence sailed to-day for the
first I time under . racing canvas, mainsail,
foresail,- jib,, jib topsail and club topsail,
and in the light breeze seemed to slip
along very fast.
The Independence will go into the navy
yard drydock to-morrow as soon as her
mast has been removed. Â¦>
New Battleship Illinois
Breaks the World's
? Record.
FASTEST CRAFT
OF HER CLASS
BOSTON, June 12.â With the proud dis
tinction of being the fastest battleship in
America and the speediest of her class in
the world, the new sea-fighter Illinois
came to' anchor in President's Roads to
night after a most successful trial. Over
the Governor course, from Cape Ann to
Cape Porpoise and return, a distance of
sixty-six nautical miles, she raced to-day
under the watchful eyes of United States
naval representatives and established a
new record by maintaining an average
speed of 17.31. knots for four consecutive
hours. Not only did she demonstrate
that she was faster than any warship of
her size afloat, but she proved her ability
to turn upon an enemy with extraordinary
quickness by describing a. complete circle
within 300 yards, or little more than twice
her length, in three minutes and ten sec
ond* w,hile plowing through the sea at
full speed.
Steadiness and reserve power character
ized the performance of the ship through
out the entire test. The work of her
engines was smooth and even and the rec
ords of the day showed but one-tenth of a
knot difference in speed between the
northern run and the return run. The
machinery easily met every requirement
and not an accident, of any kind marred
the success of the day." Â¦ 'Â¦ 'f V .
Admirals Are .'Delighted.
*Rear Admiral Sampson, who was on
board unofficially, i and Rear Admiral
Evans, who acted as president of the trial
board, as well as the other naval officers
who participated in the trial, were thor
oughly pleased with the new champion
and as she steamed back to her anchorage
with a broom at her masthead the of
ficials of the Newport News Ship-building
Company, whose three years of labor had
reached such a happy climax, were, en
thusiastically congratulated.
The day could not have been more per
fect for the trial of a battleship. 'Hardly
a breath of air ruffled the -water as the
vessel steamed out of Boston harbor. .
The Illinois was hardly a thing of beauty
as she laid her course for Cape Ann, but
the white wave turned up at her bow gave
those on- board the impression that she
was out for a business-like day's work.
The first mark-boat, the converted yacht
Hist, was in sight just before 10 o'clock,
and twelve- minutes past that hour the
ship crossed the starting line, with her
engines registering , 117 revolutions to" the
minute.
The ship passed mark-boat No. Â«2, the
training- ship -Lancaster, at a 17-knot gait,
22 minutes and 53 seconds after the start.
The next mark, the old Essex, was
picked. up 23 minutes and 20 seconds later,
the Newport in 22 minutes 12 seconds, the
Peoria in 23 minutes 3 seconds, and the
naval tug Potomac at buoy No. 6, Indi
cating the end of the 33-knot run north
ward, in 22 minutes 36 seconds. Â«' -
.Â¦ Â¦ Maintains Even Speed..
For a part of the northward run the
Illinois had reached a speed of 17.84 knots
an hour, and at no time did she drop be
low 16.97. Buoy No. 6 was passed at
12:06:07, showing elapsed time for the run
of 1 hour 45 minutes and 4 seconds, and an
average speed for the 33 knots of 17.36
nautical miles. 'â¢.Â¦'.- Â¦â¢
On the return the elapsed time between
buoys was about the same as during the
first half of the trial. Again she reached
a-maximum speed of 17.84, : with a mini
mum of 16.97.- The finish line was passed
and the speed trial was over at 1:18:33, the
elapsed time being 1:54:42 and the average
speed for the second half 17.26. 7
The completed figures for the entire 66
knots gave 3:48:46 elapsed time and the
average speed 17.31 knots per hour. Tidal
corrections may make slight alterations
in these figures, but the changes are not
likely to affect the . result' materially.

. Sixteen of the very best dogs in Amer
ica will 'compete at Union Coursing Park
on Sunday next under the auspices of the
Inter-State Coursing Club. It win be the
sixth annual ladies* day championship
stake, and will be the highest class ever
run in this State. : Among the stake win
nerd entered are For. Freedom, Palo Alto,
Ireland, Wedgewood and other famous
greyhounds. The stake Is valued at
51020. -The winner will get 1300, runner
up $200. the next two $100 each, next four
$50 each, and the balance of the money
will be, awarded as special prizes to the
dogs beaten by the winners of the first
four prizes.
The championship stake, will, bo run off
on Sunday only. A class stake, of ninety
six entries will be run on Saturday.
Coursing both days will begin on the ar
rival of the 10:15 a. m. train. Following

The Republican Primary League of San
Francisco, T. P. Woodward, president, is
rapidly gaining the favor of representa
tive men in the party. Indications point
to enrollment, under the auspices of the
league, of a very large number of Repub
lican voters. The first meeting of the di
rectors at headquarters in the Baldwin.
Annex was held last evening. Ten of the
eleven directors were present. Owing to
sickness in his family Grove P. Ayers
could not attend. The temporary officers
of the organization were declared the per
manent officers. The following standing
committees were createfl by the directors:
Financeâ Grove P. Ayera. David Rich, I. W.
Lees.
Printing and literatureâ J. George Boyne, E.
P. Iiarrett, John W. Rogers.
.Headquarters and rulesâ E. E, Bchmitz,
Janv-s P. Cheetham, L. E. Savage.
Organization and campaignâ A. Ruef. T. P.
Woodward, I. W. Lees. /
The campaign committee will meet at
headquarters every evening to confer with
district representatives. In the confer
ences officers of the leaeue other than
those on the committee will participate.
It was decided last night to appoint dis
trict organizing committees. In each dis
trict there will be a committee of five
lepresentative Republicans, whose duty It
will be to promote affiliation with the
league and awaken public interest in the
coming primary election. Much time was
Blven last evening to a discussion of by
laws. The directors expect to finish the
v.'ork in this respect and present a report
to-night.
The action of the committee in engag
ing five rooms In the Baldwin Annex for
permanent headquarters and paying five
months' rent in advance was approved by
the league. It was decided to wire the
rooms and rut in electric lights.
The league has elected' an advisory com
mittee consisting of seventy-five promi
nent citizens of Republican faith. An
nouncement of such election was not
made until yesterday afternoon, yet forty
acceptances were on file last night. It
was deemed best, however, . to withhold
publication of the names until to-morrow,
so that all acceptances may be registered
before the names are published. The pa
triotic and prompt response of . so many
leading merchants, bankers, manufac
turers and professional men assures the
promoters of the league that the move
ment is bound to be successful. . ,
Fremont Republican Club.
The Fremont Republican Club held a
large and enthusiastic meeting last even/
ing in the Maple Room of the Palace Ho
tel. Norman W. Hall presided and
speeches were made by General W.'H.'I*.
Barnes. Colonel George Stone, Dr.- Knox,
J. T. Gibson and William M. Abbott. -The
committee on new quarters reported that
suitable rooms had been found, at the
Humboldt Bank building 'and it was de
cided to allow the committee to make the
necessary arrangements to establish head
quarters there. There are now about 200
members in the club.

jr School Census Marshal Elbert yeuter
day submitted his report on the number
i of school children. in San Francisco be
; tween 5 and 17 years of age to Superin
tendent of Schools Webster. The report
shows that there are at present in this
city 82,173 children who are eligible to
attend school. This is an increase of 8619
over last year.
The State apportionment for the school
funds of this city, which Is based on the
csnsus, will amount this year to $706,
687 SO, figuring $8 CO for each school child.
Last year the apportionment, .was $679,
4f<2 10, or $8 65 for each child.
Superintendent Webster is of the opin
ion that 58 60 will be a safe estimate for
this ye.ar and the amount allowed for
schools by the State will be $27,195 70 more
than last year.
Superintendent Webster says that the
figures given are semi-official, but he
does not tnink that the official returns
will show a material difference.

SCHOOL CENSUS
SHOWS INCREASE
Marshal Elbert Registers
More Than Eighty " I
Thousand.
VT. A. Raybourn and Loretta Strove
were arrested yesterday by^Detectives
"Whittaker and McMahon as they were
about to board the Stockton steamer, and
booked en route to Perry. Oklahoma,
where they are wanted for obtaining
money by false pretenses.
From the dispatches received from the
Sheriff of Perry. Raybourn and Mrs.
Strove planned an elopement, after fraud
ulently obtaining $2000 from a real estate
dealer named Fred Cumby. Raybourn is
married to Mrs. Strove's sister; whom he
deserted to elope with Loretta. Mrs.
Strove is also married, and is the mother
of four children. The couple arrived here
last Tuesday and rented apartments in
an uptown lodging-house, registering as
L. Phebe and wife. As they intended to
leave the city they left their baggage at
the ferry, so that they could ship it at a
moment's notice. The detectives, who
were on the lookout for them, yesterday
learned where the baggage was, and they
concluded to watch it, expecting that the
couple would show ud and claim it.
Shortly after noon Raybourn and Mrs.
Strove appeared at the wharf where the
baggage was stored and ordered it shipped
to Stockton. They had already purchased
tickets for the Slough City and- were
about to board the steamer bound for
there when McMahon and Whlttaker
stepped up and placed the astonished
couple under arrest. They readily ad
mitted their identity, but denied the
charge of having defrauded the Perry real
estate dealer.
The pair will be held pending the arrival
of an officer from Oklahoma.
W. A. Raybourn .and
Loretta Strove Land
in Prison.
SCOTT LEARY, the champion ama
teur swimmer of the coast, broke
a world's record at the Olympic
Club swimming tournament last
evening. He negotiated fifty
yards In the fast time of 29 1-5 seconds
and won the race, amid the plaudits of
the large crowd that occupied seats
around the tank.
Leary's performance was notable for
the reason that he had to pass a number
of fast swimmers who had handicaps
over him. He has been develoi>ed in the
past year by Sid Cavill and is using the
double overhand stroke that won his
teacher many a contest.
A pleasing- feature of the evening's en
tertainment was the presentation of a
diamond pin to Sid Cavill, who has se
cured a leave of absence of six months in
order to recover his health. The token
was the gift of the members.
The contests were extremely interesting
and exciting. There were fancy divingv
blindfolded races, spring-board diving-,
plunging, under-watcr swimming and an
exciting life-saving exhibition by the
members of the organization. Miss Alice
Cavill and her two brothers grave an in
teresting exhibition of swimming, and the
efforts of the trio were warmly
applauded.

WASHINGTON, June 12.â Before the
Industrial Commission , this afternoon
Thomas Turnbull, representing the San
Francisco Chamber of Commerce,: testi
fied concerning labor conditions in Cali
fornia, He said that." generally speaking,
skilled labor receives better wages in
California than in the Eastern States,
where there is no similar difference in the
wages of unskilled labor. He expressed
the opinion that the cheapness of Chinese
labor ia due almost, entirely to the ineffi
ciency of the Chinese as laborers. Speak
ing of the Chinese, he said the' sentiment
in California was generally favorable to

NEW YORK, June 12.â The Herald to
morrow will say of the machinists' strike
and the National . Metal Trades Associa
tion's recent action: jj ' Â¦â¢ >
At the headquarters \ of the striking ma
chinists in 'this city yesterday a cable dis
patch was received from' the Amalgamated'
Society of Engineers In London promising
financial aid. nils society is the strongest
trades union In the world and Is said to have
over $8,000,000. In its treasury. .
â¢ The American Federation of Labor, it was
declared, will tax its 2,000,000 members '10
cents each for the Â¦ striking machinists. ;
Furthermore, it was announced last night
by the strike committee of the New York

molders, electrical workers, pattern makers,
tool makers and metal workers - ; In general
had promised \o go to the assistance of the
machinists in the event of a combination of

NEWPORT NEWS, Va,; June 12.â
The strike situation at the New
port News, Shipbuilding and Dry
dock Company's yard : Â¦ has
reached a. critical stage. In ad
dition to the striking machinists, - abouÂ±
500 in number, and the 200 skilled men
from the electrical, plumbing \and pipe
fitting departments laid off yesterday, 150
more, chiefly laborers, were laid off to
day. In each case action was due to In
terference by the strike in the progress
of the work of the departments. -Â¦> It is
said that men in other departments will
be laid off as rapidly, as they finish their
work ahead of the machinists and when
they can do no more without the aid of
the machinists. At the present rate the
yard will close down in a week..
The Business Men's Association met to
day and appointed a committee to confer
with the machinists and management of
the yard, to see If some agreement ' could
not be reached.
NEW YORK, " June 12.â The con
vention of the National Metal
Trade3 Association continued its
sessions to-day. The closing
hours of the convention-' are be
ing devoted to the completion of organi
zation and the arrangement of plans to
strengthen the hands of the manufactur
ers in their fight against the striking ma
chinists. The sum of $500,000, raised by
assessment, is to be placed at the disposal
of a strike committee to be used in be
half of the employers. Asked as to the
use of this fund W. J. Chalmers, chair
man of the press committee, said:
. I Buppose we will use It as the strikers do,'
to support their fellows, pay pickets and meet
other general expenses. We used $166,000 in
the Cleveland strike, where we paid some men
a bonus of $4 a day. There are millions more
If they are needed.
We have Just received a telegram from the
Pacific Coast pledging 114 out of 135 firms to
membership. We have delegates from San
Francisco, Seattle and Portland, and the West
is with us and we are gratified to receive their
support. We seek no trouble, but propose to
protect our interests and industries.
We are suffering no loss arising from delay
on contracts which we were filling- at the time
our machinists struck, for all of our contracts
contain a strike clause releasing us from lia
bility.
The convention decided to make no defi
nite declaration as to the length of a day's
work, leaving the question to settlement
locally. After discussing the matter all
the afternoon the following .resolution
was passed:
The declaration of principles makes the ques
tion of hours and wages a local Issue.
Resolved, That this association will support
any regular . member in his endeavor to con
form to said; declaration of principles.
"This is a victory for the declaration of
principles enunciated at Chicago," said
Chairman Chalmers of the press commit
tee. "We have stuck to that declaration
all through, and are now for it still. The
question of hours will be settled locally.!'
After passing the resolution the conven
tion adjourned and the affairs of the as
sociation with regard to the machinists'
strike are in the hands of the finance and
strike committees named this morning,
subject to the general supervision of the
administrative council.

Court Rules Out the Testimony of
One of Its Strongest Wit
nesses.
KANSAS CITY, Mo., June 12.â Judge
Wofford to-day ruled out the testimony
of one of the strongest witnesses pre
sented so far by the defense In the case
of Lulu Prince-Kennedy, on trial for the
murder of her husband. The defense had
evidently counted much upon being able
to present this testimony, and the court's
order to strike it out was considered a
blow to their side. Mrs. Kennedy was
visibly depressed by the court's action.
The witness was Edward W. Lewis, an
inspector for the Western Railway Asso
ciation Inspection Bureau, and a personal
friend of the dead man. Through him the
defense hoped to prove the theory that
Kennedy had ruined Lulu Prince under
promise of marriage and then refused
either to marry her or to live with her
after the marriage had been forced upon
him. |
The defense theory is that Kennedy
jilted Miss Prince for the Grand Rapids
woman mentioned In the statement. Other
testimony presented to-day by the de
fense â¢ was an attempt to show that
Mrs. Kennedy was temporarily insane. at
the time of the murder.
The defense had not closed when court
adjourned.

Telegraphic instructions to organize
Troops L and M of the Fifteenth Cavalry
were received yesterday at the Presidio.
Lieutenant Ward B. Persling, Fourth
Cavalry, has been assigned to duty in
connection with the organization of the
troops.
Major George S. Young and Captain El
mer W. Clark, Eighteenth Infantry, have
been ordered to the State encampment at
Santa Cruz. They will report to Major
General John H. Dickinson, division
commander, for duty with the National
Guard of California during its annual en
campment.
; A board of officers consisting of Lieu
tenant Colonel Marlon P. Maus, Inspec
tor general United States Volunteers;
Captain Alexander B. Dyer. Artillery
Corps, and Captain Eugene T. Wilson,
Artillery Corps, appointed to examine
into and report upon the origin of and re
sponsibility for the fire which destroyed a
part of the General Hospital, is in session
at the Presidio.
The junior member of the board will act
as recorder.
Captain E. Davis, Artillery Corps, has
been detached from duty at the Presidio
and ordered to Camp McKinley, Hono
lulu. He will assume command of the
post, relieving Major William Ennis.
A salute of twenty-one guns will be
fired from the batteries at the Presidio,
Alcatraz Island and Forts Baker and Ma
son at sunrise on the morning of June 17
in honor of the patriots who fell at
Bunker Hill.
MATTERS still remain the same
in* the strike of the iron work
ers. Dispatches from the East
announcing that the National
Metal Trades had decided to
raise a fund of $500,000 was not seriously
considered, for it was thought about the
various labor headquarters that this could
be easily matched by the voluntary offer
ings of workingmen and the per capita re
cently levied. The Iron Trades Council
was in session all day, but President Mc-
Cabe announced during the evening that
no change had occurred. The executive
committee of the San Francisco Labor
Council was also in session, but had no
information of any reversal of conditions.
The Wine Bottlers and Cellarmen last
night initiated ten members. They will

union was but recently organized.

The delegation from the Journeymen
Butchers' Union that went to Badeu last
night had a most enjoyable time. They
went first to Butchertown and took the
shore road southward. The men in the
abattoirs were induced to unite with their
brethren In this city in the common
cause.
Most of the shops were to some extent
prepared for the anticipated walkout and
had engaged extra men. While delays
were frequent in the large markets, the
â¢smaller shops got along fairly well. In
many instances the men were notified
that if they left after all their demands
had been granted as far as hours and
wages were concerned, their places would
be filled with others. They went out
nevertheless.
More Butchers Will Go Out.
More butchers will go out to-day. While
many men are coming into the city, few
are able to handle the business of. a
large retail shop, Â¦where alertness is re
quired. , Difficulty may also be experi
enced in securing competent slaughterers.
In this event, the slaughter-houses will
be closed down. Estimates as to the
number of men out vary. Definite reports
had not been received at the headquar
ters in the Temple.

ALTHOUGH no scarcity of meat
has as yet affected householders,
the fact that more than 1090 jour
neymen butchers left their work
yesterday morning at 10 o'clock
has greatly crippled the food supply of the
city. In some shops the men remained at
work. . These were the places where the
union card was displayed and in those
where the men had agreed to give em
ployers twenty-four hours' notice of In
tended departure.
As the men quit the bosses donned
aprons and took up the work the men had
relinquished. After a fashion customers
were served, but the prompt delivery of
goods was very problematical. As fast
as the men left the -shops they went to the
Turjc-street Temple, where they reported
to the secretary of the Journeymen
Butchers 1 Union. The heads of the or
ganization went to Baden last night to
eettle trade differences.
Xearly every place where meals are
served or meat sold was put to consider
able inconvenience. As soon as possible
pickets were established. Each one bore
a pink ribbon, on which was inscribed
"San Francisco Butchers' Union No. 115,"
with the seal of the union attached.

striking butchers the following announce
ment is made:
Approximately 1100 meat cutters and slaught
erers <iuit work. A few of our men are still
Â¦working in unfair houses â not many.

Only live of our cards have been sent back

few more. There are ISO retailers without cards.
This number Includes all the big shops, employ
ing- the most men.
Almost every union man In Butchertown Â¦went
out. and all the slaughter-houses and packing
houses are tied up. If we can keep them tied
up we have the Ftrlke won. By to-morrow all
the union men will be out. save those working
in union shons.
No butchers are coming- from the country. A
few came from Los Angeles and Portland, but
they joined our union. We have had . several
offers of dressed beef and livestock, but are not
In the buyinir business, and have passed the
offers up to the union proprietors. These oilers
car..e from the middle West. Our men are
mostly single, and they all have money. They
are prepared for a long struggle, if necessary:

NewYork Stock Market/
NEW TORK, June 12.â The stock market fell
Into remi-etagnation during the latter part of
tlie day. and the gains which were estab
lished In the morning were pretty generally
wiped out under. the driblet of Belling orders
which came /upon a market almost bare of
demand. Various points of *>trength made their
appearance during the day. but they lost their
Influence in sustaining prices. The more cheer
ful feeling in London was quite a noticeable
Influence in the open dealings. Rumors have
been very persistent for several days now that
pe&ce Is approaching in South Africa. To ap
preciate the Importance of an adjustment of
the long-drawn-out etruggle there, that is lor
the world of capital, it must be borne in mind
what enormous demands the British Govern
ment makes on accumulated capital for the
conduct of the war as well as the absolute
cutting off of an important portion of the
world's'gold product while the war lasts. The
large disbursements by the Government on war
account, combined with transfers of Russian
dcpoGlts from Paris, is what has given the
London money market its present ease and has
led to some expectation that the governors of
the Bank of England will further reduce the
minimum discount rate at to-morrow's weekly
peselon. A 10 per cent installment, amounting
to $30,000,000. of subscriptions to the -new con
Â«ol. Issue* due next week, and the -demand for
money which will result is a factor against the
reduction in the bank rate. In the local mar
ket the London influence had spent itself. The
most notable features of the market were Mis
souri Pacific and Pennsylvania, which each
rose at" much as 2% at one time. The suppo
sition ie that the Missouri Pacific directors will
meet next Monday and put the stock on a 5
per cent basis. Stocks of a<fillated companies
xhowed some . sympathy, especially the Denver
and Rio Grande stocks. The --"movement in
Pwineylvanla was due to to-day's report of
the* acquisition of the Pennsylvania Steel Com
pany by the railroad company to safeguard its
nteel rail supply. The United States Steel
rail FtockR were rather significantly heavy, but
the stocks of independent companies shot up
)n a striking manner. The movement in Colo
rado Fuel was sensational, the extreme ! rise
reaching 1Â£%, and the jumps between sales
reaching as much aa 2% points. A violent n-
