FORECAST.
WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
Generally cloudy weather prevails over the
Pacific slone except in California, where it
is clear. L'.ght scattering showers have oc
curred in the Sierras, and light rain has been
general throughout Washington, Oregon. Idaho.
Nevada and Utah. A thunderstorm is reported
from Carson City.
The pressure has risen over the country west
of the Rockies except in Arizona, where there
har been a slight fall.
The temperature has fallen decidedly over
Nevada. Utah. Southern Idaho and Northern
Arizona. There has been a slight rise in the
valleys and along the coast of California.
Conditions are favorable for fair and some
what warmer weather in California Thursday.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight June 13:
Northern Californiaâ Fair, warmer Thursday:
light northerly wind.
Southern, Californiaâ Fair Thursday; light
west wind.
Nevadaâ Partly cloudy Thursday; warmer;
light northwest wind.
Utahâ Cloudy, with showers Thursday; fresh
northwest wind.
Arizonaâ Showers in north portion; fair In
eouth portion Thursday: light northerly wind.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair, warmer
Thursday.; light northwest wind.
. G. H. WILLSON. Local Forecast Official.
Temperature 7 a. m.. 52 degrees.

Wall-Street Conditions.
The circular of Henry Clews says:
" 'Whilst the present position of affairs, is
again BUggestlve of bull tendencies, yet if\is
beset with possible complications and surprises
that counsel keeping a sharp outlook and ample
margins.
"It is hardly necessary to note the hopeful
conditions connected with the crop prospects,
the prosperous condition of home trade, the
abundance of our exports and the closing of
war conditions in the PTflltpplnes. The present
range of prices much more than discounts any
thing warranted by conditions of that char
acter. The all-controlling factor is the ex
traordinary railroad situation and especially
the nececsity arising therefrom for the pro
curement of large masses of stock for a spectal
and transient purpose. Therein lies the force
and direction of the market. It has created
a set of momentary conditions extraordinarily
conducive to speculations and has already pro
duced an elevation of prices far above the nor
mal or what can bs expected to prove per
n.anent. Yet, as matters now stand, it is
quite possible that values may rise 6till higher
and may retain the artificial advance for some
irjoderate time to come. Three p*r cent return
upon couimon stocks of railroads cannot be
counted upon as satisfactory for a perma-
THÂ»ncy, and investors may be expected "to turn
their attention later on to more remunerative
investments.
"The present market affords great facilities
fcr getting long of stocks, as tho6e who now
hold them have them for sale, but those who
buy at the present range of nrices I advise
to get out with great celerity on-the rallies."

i WEST- NEWTON,;- Pa.... June Â¦ 12.â The
death of Harry Beyeridge at t,he McKees
port faospital last night, brings the total
number of fatalities of the Port Royal
mine disaster up to eighteen. . Â¦
Another explosion took place in the
Port Royal mines at 4 o'clock this after
noon In No. 2 shaft. It sent up a whirl
wind of oil. dust and smoke and proved
conclusively that the gas Is still active
In the mines. It will probably be to-mor
row afternoon before the mine Inspectors
can get together for the purpose of decid
ing as to the advisability of making an
other attempt to recover the dead in the
mine before flooding It. Coroner Wynne
of Westmoreland County commenced an
lnquirylnto the cause of. the disaster at
this, place this afternoon, l Only, two'.wit
nesses were examined. The. Inquest will
probably occupy several flays, as the Jury,
which is composed of .well-known business
men. seems determined to get at the cause
of the disaster, \ ,'\
Rev. Father '. Carroll of , Smithton says
that some of the. men in the burning mine
are yet alive, and asked to-night that he
be permitted to lead a. relief party.
"I have four, experienced miners from
the cofce regions," he said, '.'who will po
into the shaft with me.- - I will- go ten
yards ahead and will bring out the men
allvo or dead." - . Â¦ -
St>me one spoke of removing the dIups
in the river, bed and letting the water into
the mine. . ' - '.
"That would be wrong, . said Father
Carroll.. "at least, until the mine has been
closed seven i days." .* '-<:' ....-.-. .-. Â¦ ,
Father Carroll has offered a reward of
$2000 - to : any â¢ party that.- will f recover )Â¦ the
bodies in the mine or rescue the 1 entombed
men alive. Â¦"'.'Â¦ Â¦ *â¢- : -.'-â¢'Â¦Â¦-" V- V * . -\ .,

Yow, the Chinese Consul General, was

Word was received In the Southern Pa
cific building yesterday that President
Hays, who left here Monday evening on
a tour of Inspection, will go East to
Omaha and there confer with President
Bert cf the Union Pacific.
Hays is accompanied on his trip by
General Manager Kruttschnltt. but the
latter will go no further than Ogden, and
will then return to this city.
The object of Hays' journey East to
meet Bert is only a conjecture, but those
who are on the inside claim that the two
presidents will talk over the railroad situ
ation in connection .with the new road
that Senator Clark is building between
Los Angeles and Salt Lake.
From Omaha Hays will go direct to
New York and report to Harriman.

patchers had a delightful vacation and

cJty.

James Jeffries, champion heavy-weight
pugilist of the world, is contemplating a
trip to this city with a view to signing
articles for a battle in September next.

the city last Monday, says the big fellow
wants to engage in two more contests and
then retire from, the ring. He says Jeff
ries is willing to meet Sharkey or Ruhlin,
and the local clubs will try" to arrange a
match. The San Francisco Athletic Club

. Family disturbances, culminating in the
departure of his wife and adopted daugh
ter from his home, led Almon "\Veymouth,
a pioneer contractor and mill man of
Livermore, to commit suicide at his resi
dence in that city this afternoon by shoot
ing himself in the head with a pistol while
lying on a.sofa in^the sitting-room. -._. -., Â¦_
The despondent Husband had;, put: - the <
muzzle of the weapon' in his mouth and
sent a 1 bullet crashing throughfhls brain.
His son, William, found the body shortly
after 2 o'clock, having gone to i the house
to see what detained his father from work
at the planing mill. * ' : 1."
The cause of the deed was laid bare in
a number -of letters that were found in
the house. One was addressed to Wey
mouth by his wife,' who wrote that che
had decided to leave him and that their
adopted daughter, Lulu, 17 years'bf age,,
would go with her. A postscript had
been written by the girl. In which she ex
pressed her sorrow at leaving her foster
father, but she felt compelled to depart
with her mother. The girl's action is sup
posed to have contributed mucn to the old
man's despondency, because he had a
strong affection for her. â¢ . â¢
Another note, written by Weymouth, ex
plained that he 'contemplated taking his
own life because of the desertion by hl3
wife and daughter. There were no specific
reasons given, concerning the .disagree
ments in the family, but it is understood
that relations between the various mem
bers had Â¦ been ' strained for a long time.
Mrs. Weymouth married Weymouth eight
years ago, being his second wife.^ His
children by a former marriage, a son and
a daughter, did not reside at the home
place and the townspeople have under
stood that they did not get along well
with their stepmother. r
Mrs. Weymouth and the girl left Liver
more on the morning train, saying they
intended to come to friends in Oakland.
That move settled Weymouth's determi
nation to end his existence, although he
had been thinking of suicide for several
days. Last week he tried to purchase
cartridges to fit a bulldog pistol, but
could not obtajn the size he needed. This
noon he bought a pistol and cartridges at
Dutcher's hardware shop and then went
to > his mill; where, he. attended to. some
business. â¢ .,...- ... Â¦Â» * ..." .
â¢Then the old man drove over to his resi
dence and made preparations to die.
*The dead pioneer was a native of Maine,
about 70 years of age. He settled in
Livermore Valley more than forty years
ago. He leaves one brother, who is a
rancher in the valley, and , another
brother, Albert Weymouth, manager for
the- Berkeley Lumber The
Coroner took charge of the case.

Commander B. F. Tilley, U. S. N., Gov
ernor of the American possessions In Sa
moa, and now a guest , at the Occidental
Hotel, was robbed last night of $30 and a
gold watch. He was induced by two- men
he met in the hotel lobby last night to
take a ride in a hack. He left the hotel
about 6 p. m. with the strangers, accord
ing to his story and was found six hours
later wanderings around Gray Bros.* quar
ry near the seawall.
Commander .Tilley was found at mid
night by. Arthur Flaherty and Howard
Lennox, residents of .the neighborhood,
who were attracted by the naval officer's
strange movements. Â¦ . Â¦ *
When interrogated by the police the
commander told of his . experience with
the daring, thugs. He said the men drove
him about the city, for some time, stop
ping at the > corner of Greenwich and San
some streets. about 10 p. m. .
Then they dragged him out of the. hack,
threw him to the ground and forcibly re
lieved him'of his watch and coin.
Commander Tilley appeared, when
brought to the hotel, as if he had been

His story was told incoherently and

The men were called out because they could
not handle the meat being supplied from non
union slaughter- houses. The proprietors of a
majority ot the shops axe In sympathy with
Us, however, and Instead of trying to get non
union labor in place of the union men who
walked out they are running the shops as best
they can. They have signed our agreement,
and they having done so we cannot- take the
cards away from them. Just as soon as any
attempt la mad* to secure non-union men.
however, we consider the proprietors have
broken faith and the card is withdrawn. This
stand was taken on the advice of Grand Or
ganizer Pierce, who was to conference with us
yesterday. ,
Fred Becker is trying to get men from
outside the city, but the union- represen
tatives declare he has not been success
ful. â¢ . '
"From the wholesalers' standpoint we
I feel satisfied with the situation." said
Irving C. Lewis of Grayson. Owen & Co.
[ "We expect a speedy termination of the
I strike, in view of the stand taken by the
San Francisco wholesalers."
The local\ union of the Amalgamated
Meat Cutters and -Butcher "Workmen will
hold an all-day meeting to-morrow, com
mencing at 9:30 o'clock a. m., when every
aspect of the strike will be canvassed in
detail. . Iv.'
The union reports show that James
Pitts, George Loher and M. Schlck, em
ploying butchers, have accepted the union
card. "
The press committee of the union has
issued the folio wins statement:
Every non-union shop, wholesale and
retail, has been picketed by union dele
gates. . . . â¢
The day's developments were without
significance so far as the general situa
tion goes. The wholesalers are exerting
every effort in their power to furnish beef
to the retailers who have pulled down the
union cards. To this end members of the
firms who have not slaughtered for years
are head and shoulders deep In work at
the stockyards. A few union men have
returned to work and have abandoned the
organization, but they number not a doz
en. Non-union help is being utilized wher
ever obtainable, but so far the butchers,
both wholesale and retail, have had much
difficulty in securing skilled workmen.
The union, as well as the wholesalers,
have transferred their attention In large
measure to the San Francisco side of the
bay. The strike there is of such large
proportion and the Interests so much
greater that what is accomplished by
either side Is Immediately reflected upon
the Oakland situation. The wholesalers
here are particularly watchful of the de
velopments across the bay. for. they take
their cue for the most part from the big
firms on the other side. ,

enberger was released to-day after the

son got there first and took the two shells

he put the gun back in its place and left.
When he went home he met his father
and told him. about taking the shells out
of the weapon. They then walked to the
house and ate their dinner.

HEAD OFFICERS
Membership in' Order Is
Limited to Small
Cities.
Delegates Finally Decide
Against Extending Jur
â¢â¢ - isdiction.
ST. PAUL. June 12.â Election of officers
and a protracted contest over the admis
sion of cities of over 200,000 Inhabitants to
the jurisdiction- of the order filled the
business day of the Modern Woodmen of
America to-day. The proposition to take
city members Into the order came up on
the report of the law committee. An
amendment was proposed to this report to
dispense with the present limit, which
keeps cities of over 200,000 from joining the
qrder, Chicago, St. Louis, Milwaukee. Buf
falo, Detroit, Cleveland, San Francisco,
Cincinnati, Philadelphia and Pittsburg be
ing named in several motions and . being
then taken up one at a time and voted on.
On Chicago the vote was 260 to 32S. and the
others were more quickly disposed of. all
being voted down. This leaves paragraph
4 of the raw committee's report with tn +
single recommendation that Utah be ad
mitted to the jurisdiction of the order and
this amendment will doubtless be carried
on Friday. The action this afternoon in
considered as settling for some years to
come the membership limitations of tha
Modern -Woodmen.
The first business to-day was the elec
tion of officers. Head Consul "W. A. North
cott of Illinois, Head Clerk C. W. Hawes
of Illinois and Head Adviser Dan B. Herd
of Iowa, were re-elected. R. R. Smith of
Brookfleld. Mo., was elected head banker.
Physicians were elected for each state and
the following were nominated for direct
ors, five vacancies having to be filled: Ben
jamin D.. Smith of Mankato, Minn.; J. N.
Reece of Springfield, 111.; E. E. Murphy cf
Leavenworth, Kans.; G. "W. Reilly of
Danville, -111.; C. G. Saunders of Council
Bluffs. Iowa, and T. F. Hopkins of Rock
ford, 111. .. -
After the election of Head Consul Nbrth
cott, who has served rive terms succes
sively, that official announced that he
would not again be a candidate for the
position but would retire at thÂ© end cf
the new term.
The ballot for directors, 620 delegates
voting, resulted: Hapkins. 270; Reilly. 506;
Murphy, 580; Talbot. 509; Smith, 590; Saun
ders, 564.
At the afternoon session the election of
officers was completed by the election of
the following: Head chaplain. Rev. James
I. Churm/Waupun, "Wia.; head escort. C.
D. Eliott, Washington; head watchman.
H. M. Smith, Richland, Mo.; head sentry,
A. E. Bates, Minnesota. -
Some people would rather do a thin*
twice than do it right in the first place.

The above correction is published as
news and not at the expense of the board
of directors of "the University Mound Old
Ladies' Home.
Without questioning the sincerity of the
members of the board of directors of the
University Mound Old Ladies' Home and
with keen appreciation of the worthi
ness of this charity. The Call still finds its
duty to be the publication of facts that
have come to its knoweldge which have
most vitalbearing upon the welfare of all
organized charities, of this city and its
suburbs! The "correction" Involving un
qualified indorsement of Mrs. K. C. Greg
ory, compels this paper in Justification of
its course to publish in 'further detail In
formation "in its possession as to her
methods of serving sweet charity.
Business men of San Francisco and
those charitable women who contribute
freely to the success of all efforts to raise
money for worthy charities do so with a
whole-souled liberality worthy of generous
California. In the past they have been
able to do so with a reasonable assurance
that of every dollar contributed a hundred
cents would go to relieve the suffering or
Increase the happiness of the unfortunates
who were the beneficiaries of the charity.
These people will not care to see trans
planted to this^commu-nity the pernicious
system known as "listing the town," by
which a portion, even the major part, of
money contributed may' be diverted to
the pocket of some enterprising individual,
who, without personal interest in the
charity, Is willing tc. undertake ' the un
pleasant task of management of fair or
benefit entertainment for the sake of the
profit to be made out of it. >
The Call is preoared - to prove that the
seven young women who have been selling
f:kets under the direction of Mrs. Greg
ory for the entertainment referred to in
the above "correction" are not volunteers,
but were employed by Mrs. Gregory on a
weekly wage and percentage of receipts
for all above a stipulated amount. .
An advertisement â¢ was ! inserted in the
papers, Mrs. Gregory having left the
Grand Hotel and registered at the Cali
forir.a Hotel, under the name of "Miss C.
C. Graham" for that purpose. The adver
tisements appeared Sunday -morning, and
a score or more of young women applied
In response to them. The proprietors of
the California Hotel became suspicious of
their guest when so many young women
began to. swarm Into the corridors, and
"Miss Graham" was requested to leave.
Needless to say she complied with the re
quest.
It is to be regretted that the directors
of the University Mound Old Ladles'
Home enlisted the services of a woman
who found It desirable to register under
an assumed name for purposes which
aroused suspicion, and caused her expul
sion from - a : hotel. .The home is . worthy
of aid and needs aid, and its future should
not be imperiled by. the employment ! of
such methods.

of Sunday morning aa follows: â¢

."So far as crookedness existing among
army officers is concerned, candidly 'I
don't believe there is, any." . â¢ â¢ '
The two Abrams, whose arrest has
caused all. the excitement, have been
turned over, to the Federal authorities,
and to-morrow they will be examined
before the Federal Grand Jury. Other ar
rests may. follow, but there will be found
nothing more than a multiplication of
the petty thieving that goes on wherever
troops are located.
The military investigation Is in the
hands of Lieutenant Colonel Marlon P.
Maus, inspector i general on the staff of
General Shatter. ,'
"The military authorities have now
nothing. to do with the- affair, other than
the seizure of Government goods found
in the possession of civilians, the posses
sion of which cannot be satisfactorily ac
counted for. The detection and. prosecu
tion of these dealers rests with the civil
and Federal authorities, and I hope they
make a success of it. " â¢ '..Â¦Â¦'->
"In my opinion the police and Federal
authorities are. exposing a system of pet
ty-thieving. Owing to the large number
of men these petty dealers have had op
portunity to deal with their thievery has
grown to larger proportions than.it other
wise would have.
"Notwithstanding the fact_that those
having Government ' property wrongfully
in their possession can be prosecuted on
a charge of felony, and given a sentence
of fro.m' one to five years* confinement,
there, are. plenty of men .willing to take
the chance. The goods are first-class and
can be purchased ' from the soldiers at a
ridiculously low figure. I
"The soldier cannot be prevented from
selling the stuff. . In disposing of 'it. he
leaves himself short.. The officer who is
sues to the men inquires as to the quan
tity of goods ho . has in his possession.
If the officer thinks he is amply supplied
he refuses to issue the goods the soldier
asks for. In this manner the enlisted
man is prevented from drawing more
than he needs. ' Â¦. Â¦ '-
stores "will be unearthed X>y the investiga
tion now belng'made by .Â¦the'Federal aUr
thorities. There is a strictly enforced rule
providing for the taking of stock at the
end of each month by officers held re
sponsible for stores under their, charge.
2 "I do know, " however, what a soldier
will do for a little ready money, and'I am
of the opinion that the goods found by the
police are purchased' from the soldiers
passing In and out of this post. More
than 60,000 men have passed through this
post during the war. If one man in every
ten of these soldiers sold to dealers a pair
of shoes; 600 pairs would thus be piaced
in the hands of outsiders. The same rule
applies to shirts and blankets. '

THE arrest of Lipman Abrams and
his son. George Abrams. of 1403
Powell street, Tuesday night,
marks the ' beginning Of an at
tempt on the part of the military
authorities to make the man who buys
Government "clothing from the soldier as
culpable as the soldier who sells, ahd in
that way reduce the traffic in quarter
master's stores that is going . on contin
ually. . . --
The arrest of the two men Is not the be
ginning of a great scandal, nor will gi
carrtic frauds be unearthed, nor will there
be any one beyondfa few private soldiers
and obscure merchants involved in the
trouble. The quartermaster's stores on
the . market have been secured from sol
diers who sold them in violation of the
articles of war, and it is a traffic that is
going on always at every military
poet In the - country. In the last few
months 60.000 soldiers have passed through
this port and the selling of goods by even
a very small percentage of that number
would Â«tock the market with as much
Government stuff as has ever been found
'a soldier who sells Jiis clothing is se
verely dealt with, but the military author
ities have decided to make the buyer suf
fer as much, and so the aid of the police
was called in to run down persons carry
ing on this illegal traffic and to locate all
the stolen goods possible. Detective Har-
ry Reynolds was given the task and he
has been searching for Government stuff
for several days.

riflc. he found eleven pairs of blankets,
five pairs of leggings, one blouse, ten blue

In Havana during the war the ration of
flour to each .man was one pound and a.
quarter. Â¦ The ration ' of bread . was . tlÂ»c
same. Any .baker knows that a pound of
flour will make more than a pound of
bread, and the bakers of Havana wanted
flour and the soldiers 'did not Â¦ want 1 the
trouble of. cooking bread. / It was so ar
ranged that the men drew their flour and
gave it to the bakers and the bakers fur
nished them with bread,, pound for.pound
and made the difference out of. the saving
In flour: The . market of Havana - was
flooded with Government flour and every
one knew it, a*d the traffic* was stopped
only because supplies â¢ came' in â¢ In - such;
quantities that there, was not storage
room for them all and petty thieving was
rife. The only way it could be stopped
â¢was . to keep Government supplies abso
lutely out of the market and then the man
who carried any found, trouble. .,.- J
' It is not necessary to' cancel the marks
on the cases of goods traded off by the
messes, but it isnecessary that any such
trading be done under the eye of an offi
cer, .who must certify to the transaction.
- In the navy the same | system of com
muting.'rations is going on all the time,'
but with* this difference: the navy ration
is commuted in nioney, .while that' of the
army-is in supplies. *' ' Â¦.. â¢
Flour in Havana.
The presence of 'commissary stores in
small' quantities is . easily > accounted for
and there are none in large quantities. It
is well understood -by; the commissary de
partment that 'the, men- can help out the
variety allowed In. the Government ration
by trading It. for things the Government
does riot provide. ., The army: ration/in
cludes beans, but not spinach. If the men
want spinach instead of beans they are
allowed- to- -trade 'Â¦ their- ;.ratlon: of beans
to any one who will furnish them with
spinach In exchange. , It- may not be a
profitable deal, but the -men get a phauge
in their diet; and if they are satisfied the
Government is satisfied. If. the men have
become tired of canned tomatoes, which la
part of the Government ration, they may
draw all the tomatoes coming to them
and exchange. them for fruit or butter or
eggs or any one of a dozen other things
that they like better than "Government
straight."' â¢-Â¦Â¦.-,
to any one who will buy them. They can
not use them* In. the tropics and they are
willing to let them go at any price. In
addition /men have been, known to sell
the blankets from their beds and sleep in
the cold until they had to be sent to tlis
hospital, and a pair of shoes is easily
drawn and easily, sold. .The penalty may
be confinement in the military prison, but
the men risk it' under the persuasion of
those who make 'a. '.business of living off
the troops. Had- one .-man -In ten of. all
those who have gone to. Manila' and come
from there in the last four months sold

ernment shoes in- every store 'in the city
